<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-mon-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-mon_1-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The government recognised Queen <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth II</a> as the official <a href="/wiki/Head_of_State" class="mw-redirect" title="Head of State">Head of State</a> from 1965 to 1970. The highest official of Rhodesia held the title "Officer Administering the Government" (OAtG) as he acted in lieu of the official Governor, who remained at his post but was ignored. After Rhodesia became a republic in March 1970, the President replaced the OAtG as the highest official and the Governor returned to London.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Rhodesia</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/oʊ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">oʊ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/iː/: &#39;ee&#39; in &#39;fleece&#39;">iː</span><span title="/ʒ/: &#39;s&#39; in &#39;pleasure&#39;">ʒ</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>) was an <a href="/wiki/List_of_historical_unrecognized_states_and_dependencies" title="List of historical unrecognized states and dependencies">unrecognised state</a> in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">southern Africa</a> from 1965 to 1979, equivalent in territory to modern <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>. Rhodesia was the <i>de facto</i> <a href="/wiki/Successor_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Successor state">successor state</a> to the <a href="/wiki/British_colony" class="mw-redirect" title="British colony">British colony</a> of <a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesia" title="Southern Rhodesia">Southern Rhodesia</a>, which had been <a href="/wiki/Self-governing_colony" title="Self-governing colony">self-governing</a> since achieving <a href="/wiki/Responsible_government" title="Responsible government">responsible government</a> in 1923. A <a href="/wiki/Landlocked" class="mw-redirect" title="Landlocked">landlocked</a> nation, Rhodesia was bordered by <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> to the south, <a href="/wiki/Bechuanaland_Protectorate" title="Bechuanaland Protectorate">Bechuanaland</a> (later <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a>) to the southwest, <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a> to the northwest, and <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> (<a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Mozambique" title="Portuguese Mozambique">a Portuguese province</a> until 1975) to the east.
</p><p>In the late 19th century, the territory north of the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Republic" title="South African Republic">Transvaal</a> was <a href="/wiki/Royal_charter" title="Royal charter">chartered</a> to the <a href="/wiki/British_South_Africa_Company" title="British South Africa Company">British South Africa Company</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Rhodes" title="Cecil Rhodes">Cecil Rhodes</a>. Rhodes and his <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_Column" title="Pioneer Column">Pioneer Column</a> marched north in 1890, acquiring a huge bloc of territory that <a href="/wiki/Company_rule_in_Rhodesia" title="Company rule in Rhodesia">the Company would rule</a> until the early 1920s. In 1923, the Company's charter was revoked, and Southern Rhodesia attained self-government and established a <a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesian_Legislative_Assembly" title="Southern Rhodesian Legislative Assembly">legislature</a>. Between 1953 and 1963, Southern Rhodesia was joined with <a href="/wiki/Northern_Rhodesia" title="Northern Rhodesia">Northern Rhodesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nyasaland" title="Nyasaland">Nyasaland</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Rhodesia_and_Nyasaland" title="Federation of Rhodesia and Nyasaland">Federation of Rhodesia and Nyasaland</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Decolonisation_of_Africa" title="Decolonisation of Africa">decolonisation of Africa</a> in the early 1960s alarmed a significant proportion of Rhodesia's <a href="/wiki/White_people_in_Zimbabwe" title="White people in Zimbabwe">white</a> population. In an effort to delay the transition to <a href="/wiki/No_independence_before_majority_rule" title="No independence before majority rule">black majority rule</a>, Rhodesia's predominantly <a href="/wiki/Whites_in_Zimbabwe" class="mw-redirect" title="Whites in Zimbabwe">white</a> government issued its own <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia%27s_Unilateral_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Rhodesia&#39;s Unilateral Declaration of Independence">Unilateral Declaration of Independence</a> (UDI) from the United Kingdom on 11 November 1965.  (The government of the United Kingdom supported Rhodesia's transition to a multi-racial democracy.) The UDI administration initially sought <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_recognition" title="Diplomatic recognition">recognition</a> as an autonomous <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_realm" title="Commonwealth realm">realm</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>, but reconstituted itself as a republic in 1970. The <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_Bush_War" title="Rhodesian Bush War">Rhodesian Bush War</a>, which pitted the government against two <a href="/wiki/African_nationalism" title="African nationalism">African nationalist</a> organisations, <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_African_National_Union" title="Zimbabwe African National Union">ZANU</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_African_People%27s_Union" title="Zimbabwe African People&#39;s Union">ZAPU</a>, intensified in the 1970s, prompting Rhodesian premier <a href="/wiki/Ian_Smith" title="Ian Smith">Ian Smith</a> to concede to multiracial democracy in 1978. However, a <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_Rhodesia" title="Zimbabwe Rhodesia">provisional government</a> subsequently headed by Smith and his moderate colleague <a href="/wiki/Abel_Muzorewa" title="Abel Muzorewa">Abel Muzorewa</a> failed in appeasing international critics or halting the bloodshed. By December 1979, Muzorewa had replaced Smith as <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Rhodesia" title="Prime Minister of Rhodesia">Prime Minister</a> and secured an <a href="/wiki/Lancaster_House_Agreement" title="Lancaster House Agreement">agreement</a> with the militant nationalists, allowing Rhodesia to briefly revert to colonial status pending elections under a <a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage" title="Universal suffrage">universal franchise</a>. It finally achieved internationally recognised independence in April 1980 as the <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Republic of Zimbabwe</a>.
</p><p>Rhodesia's largest cities were its capital, <a href="/wiki/Harare" title="Harare">Salisbury</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bulawayo" title="Bulawayo">Bulawayo</a>. The white population, which grew to nearly 300,000, dominated the country's politics and economy, though they never made up more than eight percent of the total population. Rhodesia developed an economy largely dependent on agriculture, manufacturing, and mining. Its largest exports were <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chrome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">tobacco</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a>. International <a href="/wiki/Economic_sanctions" title="Economic sanctions">sanctions</a> put increasing pressure on the country as time went on. The <a href="/w/index.php?title=Parliament_of_Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Parliament of Rhodesia (page does not exist)">Parliament of Rhodesia</a>, which included the lower <a href="/wiki/House_of_Assembly_(Zimbabwe)" title="House of Assembly (Zimbabwe)">House of Assembly</a> and later a <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_Zimbabwe" title="Senate of Zimbabwe">Senate</a>, was predominantly white, with minority of seats reserved for blacks. After 1970, the country used a <a href="/wiki/Semi-presidential_system" title="Semi-presidential system">semi-presidential system</a>, with a <a href="/wiki/President_of_Rhodesia" title="President of Rhodesia">President</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Rhodesia" title="Prime Minister of Rhodesia">Prime Minister</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_Rhodesia" title="Cabinet of Rhodesia">cabinet</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The official name of the country, according to the constitution adopted concurrently with the <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia%27s_Unilateral_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Rhodesia&#39;s Unilateral Declaration of Independence">Unilateral Declaration of Independence</a> (UDI) in 1965, was Rhodesia. This was not the case under British law, however, which considered the territory's legal name to be <a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesia" title="Southern Rhodesia">Southern Rhodesia</a>, the name given to the country in 1898 during the <a href="/wiki/British_South_Africa_Company" title="British South Africa Company">British South Africa Company</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Company_rule_in_Rhodesia" title="Company rule in Rhodesia">administration</a> of <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia_(region)" title="Rhodesia (region)">the Rhodesias</a>, and retained by the <a href="/wiki/Self-governing_colony" title="Self-governing colony">self-governing colony</a> of Southern Rhodesia after the end of Company rule in 1923.<sup id="cite_ref-rowland251_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rowland251-2">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This naming dispute dated back to October 1964, when <a href="/wiki/Northern_Rhodesia" title="Northern Rhodesia">Northern Rhodesia</a> became independent from Britain and concurrently changed its name to Zambia. The Southern Rhodesian colonial government in <a href="/wiki/Harare" title="Harare">Salisbury</a> felt that in the absence of a "Northern" Rhodesia, the continued use of "Southern" was superfluous. It passed legislation to become simply Rhodesia, but the British government refused to approve this on the grounds that the country's name was defined by British legislation and so could not be altered by the colonial government. Salisbury went on using the shortened name in an official manner nevertheless,<sup id="cite_ref-palley742743_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-palley742743-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> while the British government continued referring to the country as Southern Rhodesia. This situation continued throughout the UDI period.<sup id="cite_ref-rowland251_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rowland251-2">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The shortened name was used by many people including the British government in the House of Commons.
</p><p>The British government maintained this stance regarding the June–December 1979 successor state of <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_Rhodesia" title="Zimbabwe Rhodesia">Zimbabwe Rhodesia</a>, and when Zimbabwe Rhodesia returned to colonial status from December 1979 to April 1980, it was as <i>Southern Rhodesia</i>. Southern Rhodesia subsequently achieved internationally recognised independence in April 1980, when it became the Republic of Zimbabwe.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Rhodesia" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Rhodesia">History of Rhodesia</a></div>

history of Southern Rhodesia">British Dependency</a></td><td style="white-space:nowrap;text-align:right;"> 1979–1980</td></tr><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td style="text-align:left;"> <a href="/wiki/History_of_Zimbabwe" title="History of Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a></td><td style="white-space:nowrap;text-align:right;"> 1980–<span style="font-size:85%;">present</span></td></tr><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td style="text-align:left;"> <a href="/wiki/Gukurahundi" title="Gukurahundi">Gukurahundi</a></td><td style="white-space:nowrap;text-align:right;"> 1982–1987</td></tr><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td style="text-align:left;"> <i><a href="/wiki/Second_Congo_War" title="Second Congo War">Second Congo War</a></i></td><td style="white-space:nowrap;text-align:right;"> <i>1998–2003</i></td></tr></tbody></table></td>
</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top:1px solid #aaa; border-bottom:1px solid #aaa;">
<a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Zimbabwe.svg" class="image"><img alt="Flag of Zimbabwe.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Flag_of_Zimbabwe.svg/16px-Flag_of_Zimbabwe.svg.png" width="16" height="8" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Flag_of_Zimbabwe.svg/24px-Flag_of_Zimbabwe.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Flag_of_Zimbabwe.svg/32px-Flag_of_Zimbabwe.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="840" data-file-height="420" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Zimbabwe" title="Portal:Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe&#32;portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_Zimbabwe" title="Template:History of Zimbabwe"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_Zimbabwe" title="Template talk:History of Zimbabwe"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Until after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the landlocked British possession of <a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesia" title="Southern Rhodesia">Southern Rhodesia</a> was not developed as an indigenous African territory, but rather as a unique state that reflected its <a href="/wiki/Multiracialism" title="Multiracialism">multiracial</a> character.<sup id="cite_ref-duignan1986_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-duignan1986-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> This situation certainly made it very different from other lands that existed under colonial rule, as many <a href="/wiki/Europeans" class="mw-redirect" title="Europeans">Europeans</a> had arrived to make permanent homes, populating the towns as traders or settling to farm the most productive soils.<sup id="cite_ref-areahandbook_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-areahandbook-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-taylor2006_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylor2006-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> In 1922, faced with the decision to join the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_South_Africa" title="Union of South Africa">Union of South Africa</a> as a fifth province or accept nearly full internal autonomy, the electorate cast its vote against South African integration.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In view of the outcome of the referendum, the territory was annexed by the United Kingdom on 12 September 1923.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Shortly after annexation, on 1 October 1923, the first constitution for the new Colony of Southern Rhodesia came into force.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Under this constitution, Southern Rhodesia was given the right to elect its own thirty-member legislature, <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Rhodesia" title="Prime Minister of Rhodesia">premier</a>, and cabinet—although the British Crown retained a formal veto over measures affecting natives and dominated foreign policy.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Over the course of the next three decades, Southern Rhodesia experienced a degree of economic expansion and industrialisation almost unrivaled in sub-Saharan Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-Barber_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barber-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Its natural abundance of mineral wealth—including large deposits of chromium and manganese—contributed to the high rate of conventional economic growth.<sup id="cite_ref-Barber_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barber-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> However, most colonies in Africa, even those rich in natural resources, experienced difficulty in achieving similar rates of development due to a shortage of technical and managerial skills.<sup id="cite_ref-Barber_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barber-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Small, rotating cadres of colonial servants who possessed little incentive to invest their skills in the local economy were insufficient to compensate for this disadvantage.<sup id="cite_ref-Barber_18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barber-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Southern Rhodesia had negated the issue by importing a skilled workforce directly from abroad in the form of its disproportionately large European immigrant and expatriate population.<sup id="cite_ref-Barber_18-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barber-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> For example, in 1951 over 90% of white Southern Rhodesians were engaged in what the British government classified as "skilled occupations".<sup id="cite_ref-Barber_18-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barber-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> This resulted in the establishment of a diversified economy with a strong manufacturing sector and iron and steel industries.<sup id="cite_ref-duignan1986_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-duignan1986-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> As the white population increased, so too did capital imports, especially in the wake of World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-Barber_18-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barber-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The considerable investment made by European residents in the economy financed the development of Southern Rhodesia's export industries as well as the infrastructure necessary to integrate it further with international markets.<sup id="cite_ref-Barber_18-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barber-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1953, Southern Rhodesia merged with the two other British Central African states to form the <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Rhodesia_and_Nyasaland" title="Federation of Rhodesia and Nyasaland">Federation of Rhodesia and Nyasaland</a> – a loose association that placed defence and economic direction under a central government but left many domestic affairs under the control of its constituent territories.<sup id="cite_ref-weitzer1990_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weitzer1990-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> As it began to appear that decolonisation was inevitable and indigenous black populations were pressing heavily for change,<sup id="cite_ref-duignan1986_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-duignan1986-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> the federation was dissolved in 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Unilateral_Declaration_of_Independence_.281965.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Unilateral_Declaration_of_Independence_(1965)">Unilateral Declaration of Independence (1965)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Unilateral Declaration of Independence (1965)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although prepared to grant formal independence to Southern Rhodesia (now Rhodesia), the British government had adopted a policy of <i><a href="/wiki/No_independence_before_majority_rule" title="No independence before majority rule">no independence before majority rule</a></i>, dictating that colonies with a substantial population of European settlers would not receive independence except under conditions of <a href="/wiki/Majority_rule" title="Majority rule">majority rule</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> White Rhodesians initially balked at the suggestion; some felt they had a right to absolute political control, at least for the time being, despite their relatively small numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-weitzer1990_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weitzer1990-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The Rhodesian authorities were also disturbed by the post-independence chaos that was plaguing other African nations at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-SmithGB_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SmithGB-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> However, once Rhodesia had been introduced as a topic for discussion in international bodies, extension of the status quo became a matter of concern to the world community and a serious embarrassment to the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-areahandbook_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-areahandbook-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After the federal break-up in 1963, then-Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Alec_Douglas-Home" title="Alec Douglas-Home">Alec Douglas-Home</a> insisted that preconditions on independence talks hinge on what he termed the "five principles" – unimpeded progress to majority rule, assurance against any future legislation decidedly detrimental to black interests, "improvement in the <a href="/wiki/Civil_and_political_rights" title="Civil and political rights">political status</a>" of local Africans, moves towards ending <a href="/wiki/Racial_discrimination" title="Racial discrimination">racial discrimination</a>, and agreement on a settlement that could be "acceptable to the whole population".<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a> and his incoming <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a> government took an even harder line on demanding that these points be legitimately addressed before an independence agenda could be set.<sup id="cite_ref-areahandbook_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-areahandbook-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1964, growing dissatisfaction with the ongoing negotiations ousted Salisbury's incumbent <a href="/wiki/Winston_Field" title="Winston Field">Winston Field</a>, replacing him with <a href="/wiki/Ian_Smith" title="Ian Smith">Ian Smith</a>, deputy chairman of the conservative <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_Front" title="Rhodesian Front">Rhodesian Front</a> party.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Smith, the colony's first Rhodesian-born leader, soon came to personify resistance to liberals in British government and those agitating for change at home.<sup id="cite_ref-areahandbook_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-areahandbook-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In September 1964, Smith visited Lisbon, where Portuguese prime minister <a href="/wiki/Ant%C3%B3nio_de_Oliveira_Salazar" title="António de Oliveira Salazar">António de Oliveira Salazar</a> promised him "maximum support" if he should declare independence.<sup id="cite_ref-Onslow_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Onslow-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Aside from a common interest in maintaining security ties in southern Africa, Salazar expressed a great deal of anger at Britain's refusal to support Portugal when <a href="/wiki/Annexation_of_Goa" title="Annexation of Goa">India seized Goa</a> in 1961, admonishing Smith not to trust the British government.<sup id="cite_ref-Onslow_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Onslow-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> A Rhodesian Trade Office was opened in Lisbon in order to co-ordinate breaking the anticipated sanctions in the event of a unilateral declaration of independence later that year, which encouraged Smith not to compromise.<sup id="cite_ref-Onslow_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Onslow-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> In its turn, the Rhodesian Trade Office in Lisbon functioned as a <i>de facto</i> embassy and caused tension with London, which objected to Rhodesia conducting its own foreign policy.<sup id="cite_ref-Onslow_35-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Onslow-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> As land-locked Rhodesia bordered on the Portuguese colony of Mozambique, Salazar's promise of "maximum support" from Portugal in breaking the anticipated sanctions gave Smith more grounds for self-confidence in his talks with London.<sup id="cite_ref-Onslow_35-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Onslow-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Smith ruled out acceptance for all five of the proposed principles as they stood,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> implying instead that Rhodesia was already legally entitled to independence—a claim that was overwhelmingly endorsed by registered voters in a <a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesian_independence_referendum,_1964" title="Southern Rhodesian independence referendum, 1964">referendum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Emboldened by the results of this referendum and the subsequent general election, Rhodesia now threatened to assume her own sovereignty without British consent. Harold Wilson countered by warning that such an irregular procedure would be considered <a href="/wiki/Treason" title="Treason">treasonous</a>, although he specifically rejected using armed force against the English "kith and kin" in Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-guardian.co.uk_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian.co.uk-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Wilson's refusal to consider a military option encouraged Smith to proceed with his plans. Talks quickly broke down, and final efforts in October to achieve a settlement floundered; the Rhodesian Front remained unwilling to accept what were regarded as unacceptably drastic terms and the British would settle for nothing less – it was a formula doomed to failure.<sup id="cite_ref-areahandbook_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-areahandbook-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Udi2-rho.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a9/Udi2-rho.jpg/220px-Udi2-rho.jpg" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a9/Udi2-rho.jpg/330px-Udi2-rho.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/a/a9/Udi2-rho.jpg 2x" data-file-width="348" data-file-height="269" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Udi2-rho.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ian_Smith" title="Ian Smith">Ian Smith</a> signs the Unilateral Declaration of Independence.</div></div></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The mantle of the pioneers has fallen on our shoulders to sustain civilisation in a primitive country.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Ian Smith, 11 November 1965, upon the announcement of UDI<sup id="cite_ref-laager_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-laager-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>On 11 November 1965, following a brief but solemn <a href="/wiki/Consensus_decision-making" title="Consensus decision-making">consensus</a>, Rhodesia's leading statesmen issued a <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia%27s_Unilateral_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Rhodesia&#39;s Unilateral Declaration of Independence">unilateral declaration of independence</a> (UDI).<sup id="cite_ref-duignan1986_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-duignan1986-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-raft_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-raft-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-blackfire_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blackfire-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> This was immediately denounced as an "act of rebellion against the Crown" in the United Kingdom, and Wilson promised that the illegal action would be short-lived.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> However, few seemed to initially realise that Rhodesia was no longer within the Commonwealth's direct sphere of influence and British rule was now a constitutional fiction; Salisbury remained virtually immune to credible metropolitan leverage.<sup id="cite_ref-weitzer1990_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weitzer1990-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 12 October 1965, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a> had noted the repeated threats of the Rhodesian authorities "to declare unilaterally the independence of Southern Rhodesia, in order to perpetuate minority rule", and called upon Wilson to use all means at his disposal (including military force) to prevent the Rhodesian Front from asserting independence.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> After UDI was proclaimed, UN officials branded Ian Smith's government as an "illegal racist minority regime"<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> and called on member states to sever economic ties with Rhodesia, recommending <a href="/wiki/International_sanctions" title="International sanctions">sanctions</a> on petroleum products and military hardware.<sup id="cite_ref-areahandbook_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-areahandbook-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In December 1966, these measures became mandatory, extending to bar the purchase of Rhodesian tobacco, chrome, copper, asbestos, sugar, meat, and hides.<sup id="cite_ref-areahandbook_5-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-areahandbook-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The UK, having already adopted extensive sanctions of its own, <a href="/wiki/Beira_Patrol" title="Beira Patrol">dispatched</a> a <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> squadron to monitor oil deliveries in the port of <a href="/wiki/Beira,_Mozambique" title="Beira, Mozambique">Beira</a> in Mozambique, from which a strategic pipeline ran to <a href="/wiki/Mutare" title="Mutare">Umtali</a> in Rhodesia. The warships were to deter "by force, if necessary, vessels reasonably believed to be carrying oil destined for (Southern) Rhodesia".<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gowlland-debbas1990_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gowlland-debbas1990-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some nations, such as <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a>, which were not UN members, conducted business legally with Rhodesia – the latter remained the Smith government's largest trading partner in Western Europe until 1973, when Bonn joined the UN.<sup id="cite_ref-areahandbook_5-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-areahandbook-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Japan continued to accept more Rhodesian exports than any other nation, and <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> provided oil.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The Portuguese government marketed Rhodesian products as its own, via false <a href="/wiki/Certificate_of_origin" title="Certificate of origin">certificates of origin</a> and disguised trade channels.<sup id="cite_ref-okoth2006_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-okoth2006-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> South Africa openly refused to observe the UN sanctions.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Byrd_Amendment_(1971)" title="Byrd Amendment (1971)">1971 amendment</a> passed in the United States permitted American firms to go on importing Rhodesian chromium and nickel as normal.<sup id="cite_ref-meredith_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meredith-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite the poor showing of sanctions, Rhodesia found it nearly impossible to obtain diplomatic recognition abroad. In 1970, the US government had made it clear that the UDI would not be recognised "under [any] circumstances".<sup id="cite_ref-a_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-a-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Even the <a href="/wiki/National_Party_(South_Africa)" title="National Party (South Africa)">National Party</a> government in South Africa, although sympathetic, did not recognise Rhodesia as an independent state, maintaining only an Accredited Diplomatic Representative in <a href="/wiki/Harare" title="Harare">Salisbury</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-google13_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google13-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> This allowed <a href="/wiki/Pretoria" title="Pretoria">Pretoria</a> to continue to recognise British sovereignty as well as to deal with the <i>de facto</i> authority of the Smith government<sup id="cite_ref-google257_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google257-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Initially, the state retained its pledged loyalty to <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabeth II of the United Kingdom">Elizabeth II of the United Kingdom</a>, recognising her as Queen of Rhodesia.<sup id="cite_ref-areahandbook_5-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-areahandbook-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> When Smith and Deputy Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Clifford_Dupont" title="Clifford Dupont">Clifford Dupont</a> called on colonial Governor Sir <a href="/wiki/Humphrey_Gibbs" title="Humphrey Gibbs">Humphrey Gibbs</a> to formally notify him of the UDI, Gibbs condemned the UDI as an act of treason. After Smith formally announced the UDI on the radio, Gibbs used his <a href="/wiki/Reserve_power" title="Reserve power">reserve power</a> to dismiss Smith and his entire cabinet from office on orders from Whitehall. However, Gibbs was unable to enact any concrete actions to foster a return to legality. Government ministers simply ignored his notices, contending that UDI made his office obsolete. Even so, Gibbs continued to occupy his residence in Salisbury until 1970, when he vacated the premises and left Rhodesia following the declaration of a republic.<sup id="cite_ref-Age_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Age-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> He had effectively been superseded before then; the Smith government stated that if the Queen did not appoint a Governor-General, it would name Dupont as "<a href="/wiki/Officer_Administering_the_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="Officer Administering the Government">Officer Administering the Government</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-strips_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-strips-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Smith had intended to have Dupont named Governor-General, but Elizabeth would not even consider this advice. With few exceptions, the international community backed Whitehall's assertion that Gibbs was the Queen's only legitimate representative, and hence the only lawful authority in what it still maintained was <i>Southern</i> Rhodesia.
</p><p>In September 1968, the <a href="/wiki/Appellate_court" title="Appellate court">Appellate Division</a> of the Rhodesian High Court ruled that Ian Smith's administration had become the <i>de jure</i> government of the country, not merely the <i>de facto</i> one.<sup id="cite_ref-Beadle_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beadle-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> To support his decision, Chief Justice <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Beadle" title="Hugh Beadle">Sir Hugh Beadle</a> used several statements made by <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Grotius" title="Hugo Grotius">Hugo Grotius</a>, who maintained that there was no way that a nation could rightly claim to be governing a particular territory – if it was waging a war against that territory. Beadle argued that due to Britain's economic war against Rhodesia, she could not (at the same point) be described as <i>governing</i> Rhodesia.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Resulting court decisions held that the Smith government "could lawfully do anything its predecessors could lawfully have done".<sup id="cite_ref-legalitylaw_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-legalitylaw-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A Salisbury commission chaired by prominent lawyer W.R. Waley was appointed to study constitutional options open to the Rhodesian authorities as of April 1968, but reaching a further settlement with the British was ruled out early on.<sup id="cite_ref-weitzer1990_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weitzer1990-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Waley, although insistent that "Europeans must surrender any belief in permanent European domination", also testified that majority rule was not desirable immediately.<sup id="cite_ref-areahandbook_5-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-areahandbook-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Talks aimed at easing the differences between Rhodesia and the United Kingdom were carried out aboard Royal Navy vessels once in December 1966 and again in October 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Both efforts failed to achieve agreement, although Harold Wilson added a sixth principle to the five he had previously enunciated: "it would be necessary to ensure that, regardless of race, there was no oppression of the majority by the minority or of [any] minority by the majority." Rhodesian resolve stiffened following a failure to reach a new settlement, with more radical elements of the Rhodesian Front calling for a republican constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-areahandbook_5-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-areahandbook-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During a <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_constitutional_referendum,_1969" title="Rhodesian constitutional referendum, 1969">two-proposition referendum</a> held in 1969, the proposal for severing all remaining ties to the British Crown passed by a majority of 61,130 votes to 14,327.<sup id="cite_ref-areahandbook_5-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-areahandbook-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Rhodesia declared itself a republic on 2 March 1970. Under the new constitution, a president served as ceremonial head of state, with the prime minister nominally reporting to him.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Some in Rhodesian government had hoped in vain that the declaration of a republic would finally prompt other nations to grant recognition.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_of_UDI">Impact of UDI</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Impact of UDI">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The years following Rhodesia's UDI saw an unfolding series of economic, military, and political pressures placed on the country that eventually brought about majority rule, a totality of these factors rather than any one the reason for introducing change.<sup id="cite_ref-frontiersmen_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frontiersmen-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> In 2005, a conference at the <a href="/wiki/London_School_of_Economics" title="London School of Economics">London School of Economics</a> that discussed Rhodesia's independence concluded that UDI was sparked by an existing racial conflict complicated by <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> intrigues.<sup id="cite_ref-b_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Critics of UDI sought to maintain that Ian Smith intended only to safeguard the privileges of an entrenched colonial elite at the expense of the impoverished African community. According to this logic, UDI created a vacuum of oppression that was eventually filled by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Mugabe" title="Robert Mugabe">Robert Mugabe</a>'s dictatorship.<sup id="cite_ref-c_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-c-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Smith and his supporters continued to defend their actions, however, by claiming that the Rhodesian majority was too inexperienced at the time to manage what was, by contemporary African standards, a reasonably industrialised nation.<sup id="cite_ref-SmithGB_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SmithGB-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At large, the European population's emerging attitude to UDI was tense. Many white Rhodesians had seen themselves as nothing less than fully fledged members of the British Empire, carrying on the same rugged values and frontier spirit of the early Englishmen who had settled in 1890.<sup id="cite_ref-raft_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-raft-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> But such confidence was rudely shaken by Whitehall's refusal to grant independence on their terms. After 1965, there were those who continued to claim that they were collectively upholders of principle and defenders of such values against the twin threats of communism, manifested through the militant black nationalists, and – ironically – the <a href="/wiki/Decadence" title="Decadence">decadence</a> of Britain herself.<sup id="cite_ref-raft_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-raft-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Often repeated appeals to the Christian heritage of their pioneer ancestors in "defending the <a href="/wiki/Free_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Free world">free world</a>" reflected these beliefs.<sup id="cite_ref-raft_43-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-raft-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>African parties displayed initial horror at Smith's declaration, with one <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_African_National_Union" title="Zimbabwe African National Union">ZANU</a> official stating, "...for all those who cherish freedom and a meaningful life, UDI has set a collision course that cannot be altered. 11 November 1965 [has] marked the turning point of the struggle for freedom in that land from a constitutional and political one to primarily a military struggle."<sup id="cite_ref-blackfire_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blackfire-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> It would, however, be several years before even the most radical nationalists chose to develop a coherent strategy revolving around armed resistance, preferring instead to create opportunities for <a href="/wiki/Interventionism_(politics)" title="Interventionism (politics)">external intervention</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-blackfire_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blackfire-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Because Rhodesian exports were generally competitive and had previously been entitled to preferential treatment on the British market, the former colony did not recognise the need for escalating the pace of <a href="/wiki/Diversification_(marketing_strategy)" title="Diversification (marketing strategy)">diversification</a> before independence. Following the UDI, however, Rhodesia began to demonstrate that it had the potential to develop a greater degree of economic <a href="/wiki/Self-sufficiency" class="mw-redirect" title="Self-sufficiency">self-sufficiency</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SmithGB_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SmithGB-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> After the Rhodesian Front began introducing incentives accorded to domestic production, industrial output expanded dramatically. A rigid system of countermeasures enacted to combat sanctions succeeded in blunting their impact for at least a decade.<sup id="cite_ref-areahandbook_5-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-areahandbook-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Over the next nine years Rhodesian companies, spiting the <a href="/wiki/International_asset_recovery" title="International asset recovery">freezing of their assets and blocking of overseas accounts</a>, also perfected cunning techniques of sanctions evasion through both local and foreign subsidiaries, which operated on a clandestine trade network.<sup id="cite_ref-areahandbook_5-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-areahandbook-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From 1968 until 1970, there was virtually no further dialogue between Rhodesia and the UK. In a <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_constitutional_referendum,_1969" title="Rhodesian constitutional referendum, 1969">referendum</a> in 1969, white voters approved a new constitution and the establishment of a republic, thereby severing Rhodesia's last links with the British Crown, duly declared in March 1970. This changed immediately after the election of <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a>, who reopened negotiations.<sup id="cite_ref-racewar_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-racewar-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Smith remained optimistic that Heath would do his utmost to remedy Anglo-Rhodesian relations, although disappointed that he continued to adhere publicly to the original "five principles" proposed by Alec Douglas-Home, now <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Affairs" title="Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs">foreign secretary</a>. In November 1971, Douglas-Home renewed contacts with Salisbury and announced a proposed agreement that would be satisfactory to both sides – it recognised Rhodesia's 1969 constitution as the legal frame of government, while agreeing that gradual legislative representation was an acceptable formula for unhindered advance to majority rule.<sup id="cite_ref-areahandbook_5-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-areahandbook-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, the new settlement, if approved, would also implement an immediate improvement in black political status, offer a means to terminate racial discrimination, and provide a solid guarantee against retrogressive constitutional amendments.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Implementation of the proposed settlement hinged on popular acceptance, but the Rhodesian government consistently refused to submit it to a universal referendum.<sup id="cite_ref-areahandbook_5-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-areahandbook-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> A twenty four-member commission headed by an eminent jurist, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Pearce,_Baron_Pearce" title="Edward Pearce, Baron Pearce">Lord Pearce</a>, was therefore tasked with ascertaining <a href="/wiki/Public_opinion" title="Public opinion">public opinion</a> on the subject.<sup id="cite_ref-historyps_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historyps-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> In 1972, the commission began interviewing interest groups and sampling opinions – although concern was expressed over the widespread <a href="/wiki/Political_apathy" title="Political apathy">apathy</a> encountered.<sup id="cite_ref-SmithGB_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SmithGB-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> According to the commission, whites were in favour of the settlement, and Rhodesians of <a href="/wiki/Coloured" class="mw-redirect" title="Coloured">Coloured</a> or Asian ancestry generally pleased, while the black response to the settlement's terms was resoundingly negative.<sup id="cite_ref-racewar_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-racewar-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> As many as thirty black Rhodesian chiefs and politicians voiced their opposition, prompting Britain to withdraw from the proposals on the grounds of the commission's report.<sup id="cite_ref-historyps_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historyps-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Bush_War">The Bush War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The Bush War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_Bush_War" title="Rhodesian Bush War">Rhodesian Bush War</a></div>
<p>As early as 1960, <a href="/wiki/Minority_rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Minority rule">minority rule</a> in Southern Rhodesia was already being challenged by a rising tide of <a href="/wiki/Political_violence" title="Political violence">political violence</a> led by African nationalists such as <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Nkomo" title="Joshua Nkomo">Joshua Nkomo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ndabaningi_Sithole" title="Ndabaningi Sithole">Ndabaningi Sithole</a>. After their public campaigns were initially suppressed, many believed that negotiation was completely incapable of meeting their aspirations. Petrol bombings by radicals became increasingly common, with the <i>Zimbabwe Review</i> observing in 1961, "for the first time home-made petrol bombs were used by freedom fighters in Salisbury against settler establishments."<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> It was officially noted that between January and September 1962 alone, 33 bombings were carried out, in addition to 27 acts of attempted sabotage on communications. In that same period, nationalists were implicated in arson targeting 18 schools and 10 churches.<sup id="cite_ref-blackfire_44-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blackfire-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Nkomo's <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_African_People%27s_Union" title="Zimbabwe African People&#39;s Union">Zimbabwe African People's Union</a> (ZAPU) subsequently disclosed that it had formed a military wing, the <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_People%27s_Revolutionary_Army" title="Zimbabwe People&#39;s Revolutionary Army">Zimbabwe People's Revolutionary Army</a> (ZIPRA), and 'the decision to start bringing in arms and ammunition and to send young men away for sabotage training' had already been made. The Rhodesian authorities responded by <a href="/wiki/Ban_(law)" title="Ban (law)">banning</a> ZAPU and driving its supporters underground.<sup id="cite_ref-crisis_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crisis-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Frustrated by their repeated failures, nationalists also conducted a campaign of terror against black Africans, murdering those who had either identified with the colonial administration or had simply failed to demonstrate their allegiance to the cause.<sup id="cite_ref-blackfire_44-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blackfire-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> To protect civilians, emergency laws were imposed, broadening the legal definition of unlawful gatherings and giving the police greater powers to crack down on agitators or subversives.<sup id="cite_ref-Emergency_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emergency-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment" title="Capital punishment">death sentence</a> was also introduced for terrorism involving explosives and arson.<sup id="cite_ref-areahandbook_5-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-areahandbook-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A crisis of confidence soon resulted across ZAPU, which was already suffering from poor morale, compounded by tribal and ideological factionalism. In 1963, party dissidents rejected Joshua Nkomo's authority and formed their own organisation, the <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_African_National_Union" title="Zimbabwe African National Union">Zimbabwe African National Union</a> (ZANU) – which worked out its own strategy for impressing international opinion, undermining white assurance, and achieving a complete breakdown of order. By August 1964, ZANU was banned by the Rhodesian government as well, which cited widespread intimidation by that party.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>ZANU's agenda was inward-looking, leftist, and <a href="/wiki/Pan-Africanism" title="Pan-Africanism">pan-Africanist</a> in nature. Ndabaningi Sithole and avowed Marxist Robert Mugabe, its most prominent leaders, demanded a one party Zimbabwean state with majority rule and a public monopoly on land.<sup id="cite_ref-areahandbook_5-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-areahandbook-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> After being forced from Rhodesia, they continued to operate in exile, creating occupation groups representing urban workers, miners, and peasant farmers. ZANU also attracted professionals, students, and feminists to its ranks. While ZAPU theoretically continued to command the allegiance of most <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ndebele_people" title="Northern Ndebele people">Ndebele</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shona_people" title="Shona people">Shona</a> activists, Sithole and Mugabe drew their support base from the rural peasantry in the <a href="/wiki/Mashonaland" title="Mashonaland">Mashonaland</a> countryside.<sup id="cite_ref-areahandbook_5-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-areahandbook-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After the UDI, ZANU officials mapped an elaborate plan for the "liberation of Zimbabwe" which called for attacks on white farmers, destruction of cash crops, disrupting electricity in urban areas, and petrol bombings.<sup id="cite_ref-blackfire_44-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blackfire-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> They also formed an armed wing of their own, the <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_African_National_Liberation_Army" title="Zimbabwe African National Liberation Army">Zimbabwe African National Liberation Army</a> (ZANLA).<sup id="cite_ref-frontiersmen_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frontiersmen-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sithole and Nkomo both insisted on the need for armed struggle, but disagreed on the means to go about it. For example, ZIPRA tended to follow Soviet thinking, placing an emphasis on sophisticated weaponry in the hopes of winning a conventional battle like the <a href="/wiki/Viet_Minh" class="mw-redirect" title="Viet Minh">Viet Minh</a> at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dien_Bien_Phu" title="Battle of Dien Bien Phu">Dien Bien Phu</a>. ZANLA militants preferred to politicise populations in areas which they intended to seize.<sup id="cite_ref-frontiersmen_71-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frontiersmen-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Neither force, however, had acquired basic knowledge of guerrilla warfare. Debate on political theory and insurgent tactics became the obsession of nationalists at this stage.<sup id="cite_ref-blackfire_44-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blackfire-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scoutwithgun.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Scoutwithgun.jpg/220px-Scoutwithgun.jpg" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Scoutwithgun.jpg/330px-Scoutwithgun.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a6/Scoutwithgun.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="256" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scoutwithgun.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Rhodesian soldier interrogates villagers in late 1977 at gunpoint. This photograph would become one of the most enduring images of the bush war.</div></div></div>
<p>In April 1966, two ZANLA units, having received prior training at <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> Military College, crossed into Rhodesia from Zambia. They were armed with <a href="/wiki/SKS" title="SKS">SKS</a> carbines, hand grenades, explosives, and communist pamphlets, having been issued vague instructions to sabotage important installations before killing white persons indiscriminately.<sup id="cite_ref-blackfire_44-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blackfire-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> At least five guerrillas were simply arrested before getting very far. Another seven hoped to destroy a <a href="/wiki/Electricity_pylon" class="mw-redirect" title="Electricity pylon">pylon</a> carrying electricity to <a href="/wiki/Chinhoyi" title="Chinhoyi">Sinoia</a> in the northwest. Their faulty demolitions were uncovered by the <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_Security_Forces" title="Rhodesian Security Forces">Rhodesian Security Forces</a> and the men easily tracked to a nearby ranch on 28 April, where they were shot resisting capture.<sup id="cite_ref-frontiersmen_71-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frontiersmen-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-military_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-military-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> This event is considered to have been the first engagement of what came to be known as the "Bush War" in Rhodesia and the "Second <i lang="sn-Latn" title="Shona language text">Chimurenga</i>" (or <i>rebellion</i> in <a href="/wiki/Shona_language" title="Shona language">Shona</a>) by supporters of the guerrillas.<sup id="cite_ref-sechim_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sechim-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The campaign proper is generally considered to have started in 1972 with the <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Altena_Farm" title="Attack on Altena Farm">Attack on Altena Farm</a>, despite the minor threat already represented by the nationalist movements in the 1960s.
</p><p>After unsuccessful appeals to Britain and the United States for military assistance, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Mugabe" title="Robert Mugabe">Robert Mugabe</a>, who was based in Mozambique after that country's independence from Portugal in 1975, led ZANU to seek support from the People's Republic of China and countries of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Bloc" class="mw-redirect" title="Soviet Bloc">Soviet Bloc</a>. <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Nkomo" title="Joshua Nkomo">Joshua Nkomo</a>, based in <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a> and also supported by the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, led ZAPU.<sup id="cite_ref-impression_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-impression-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> ZANU and ZAPU together formed 'the Patriotic Front'. Broadly, ZANLA recruited mainly from Mashonaland and Manicaland provinces, whilst the ZIPRA recruited from Mashonaland West, Midlands and Matabeleland provinces of Zimbabwe. As Mugabe had described himself in an interview as a "Marxist-Leninist of Maoist Thought", which enraged the Kremlin, Soviet support went exclusively to ZAPU while China supported ZANU.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated465_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated465-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Soviet arms went to the ZAPU via Zambia and Mozambique, and Nkomo was in regular contact with Vasili Grigoryevich Solodovnikov, the Soviet ambassador to Zambia who was also known to be associated with the KGB.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated465_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated465-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Nkomo, who depended heavily on Soviet arms, had what he called an "extensive correspondence" with Yuri Andropov, the KGB chief, while officers from the Cuban DGI provided training for the ZAPU.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated465_87-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated465-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After the collapse of Portuguese rule in Mozambique in 1974–75, it was no longer viable for the Smith regime to sustain white <a href="/wiki/Minority_rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Minority rule">minority rule</a> indefinitely. By this time, even South Africa's Vorster had come to this view. While Vorster was unwilling to make concessions to his own country's blacks, he concluded that white minority rule was not sustainable in a country where blacks outnumbered whites 22:1.<sup id="cite_ref-impression_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-impression-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> In 1978, there were 270,000 Rhodesians of European descent and more than six million Africans.<sup id="cite_ref-desc_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-desc-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>International business groups involved in the country (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Lonrho" title="Lonrho">Lonrho</a>) transferred their support from the Rhodesian government to black nationalist parties. Business leaders and politicians feted Nkomo on his visits to Europe. ZANU also attracted business supporters who saw the course that future events were likely to take.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Funding and arms support provided by supporters, particularly from the Soviet Union and its allies in the latter 1970s, allowed both ZIPRA and the ZANLA to acquire more sophisticated weaponry, thereby increasing the military pressure that the guerrillas were able to place on Rhodesia.
</p><p>Until 1972, containing the guerrillas was little more than a police action. Even as late as August 1975 when Rhodesian government and black nationalist leaders met <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Falls_Conference_(1975)" title="Victoria Falls Conference (1975)">at Victoria Falls</a> for negotiations brokered by South Africa and Zambia, the talks never got beyond the procedural phase.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Rhodesian representatives made it clear they were prepared to fight an all out war to prevent majority rule.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> However, the situation changed dramatically after the end of Portuguese colonial rule in Mozambique in 1975. Rhodesia now found itself almost entirely surrounded by hostile states and even South Africa, its only real ally, pressed for a settlement.
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Having let slip one chance after another of reaching an accommodation with more moderate black leaders, Rhodesia's whites seem to have made the tragic choice of facing black nationalism over the barrel of a gun rather than the conference table. The downhill road toward a race war in Rhodesia is becoming increasingly slippery with blood.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite><i><a href="/wiki/Rand_Daily_Mail" class="mw-redirect" title="Rand Daily Mail">Rand Daily Mail</a></i> editorial, May 1976<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>At this point, ZANU's alliance with <a href="/wiki/FRELIMO" title="FRELIMO">FRELIMO</a> (the Liberation Front of Mozambique) and the porous border between Mozambique and eastern Rhodesia enabled large-scale training and infiltration of ZANU/ZANLA fighters. The governments of Zambia and Botswana were also emboldened sufficiently to allow resistance movement bases to be set up in their territories. Guerrillas began to launch operations deep inside Rhodesia, attacking roads, railways, economic targets and isolated security force positions, in 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rhodesian_Army_recruitment_Bush_War_1976.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0c/Rhodesian_Army_recruitment_Bush_War_1976.png/220px-Rhodesian_Army_recruitment_Bush_War_1976.png" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0c/Rhodesian_Army_recruitment_Bush_War_1976.png/330px-Rhodesian_Army_recruitment_Bush_War_1976.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/0/0c/Rhodesian_Army_recruitment_Bush_War_1976.png 2x" data-file-width="339" data-file-height="254" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rhodesian_Army_recruitment_Bush_War_1976.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rhodesian servicewoman takes aim with her <a href="/wiki/Browning_Hi-Power" title="Browning Hi-Power">Browning Hi-Power</a> <a href="/wiki/9%C3%9719mm" class="mw-redirect" title="9×19mm">9×19mm</a> <a href="/wiki/Semi-automatic_pistol" title="Semi-automatic pistol">semi-automatic pistol</a>; from a 1976 army recruitment poster</div></div></div>
<p>The government adopted a 'strategic hamlets' policy of the kind used in <a href="/wiki/Malayan_Emergency#Comparisons_with_Vietnam" title="Malayan Emergency">Malaya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam</a> to restrict the influence of insurgents over the population of rural areas. Local people were forced to relocate to protected villages (PVs) which were strictly controlled and guarded by the government against rebel atrocities. The protected villages were compared by the guerrillas to <a href="/wiki/Concentration_camps" class="mw-redirect" title="Concentration camps">concentration camps</a>. Some contemporary accounts claim that this interference in the lives of local residents induced many of them who had previously been neutral to support the guerrillas.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The war degenerated into rounds of increasing brutality from all three parties involved (ZANU and ZAPU, and the Rhodesian Army). Mike Subritzky, a former <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">NZ</a> Army ceasefire monitor in Rhodesia, in 1980 described the war as "both bloody and brutal and brought out the very worst in the opposing combatants on all three sides."<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A major problem for the Rhodesian state in fighting the Bush War was always a shortage of manpower.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated6_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated6-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Of the 3,000 white men liable for conscription in 1973, only about 1,000 reported when called-up.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated6_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated6-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> In February 1978, the Rhodesian Army stated it needed a minimum of 1,041 men to continue combat operations, and of those called up, only 570 reported for duty while the rest chose to move to South Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated6_96-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated6-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> The Rhodesian Army consistently out-fought the ZANU and ZAPU guerillas. However, white emigration caused a shortage of military manpower. White emigration increased as the state called up more and more men to fight in the war, creating a vicious circle, which gradually limited the capacity of the Rhodesian state to continue the war.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> In order to stop white emigration, the Smith government brought in a law in 1975 forbidding Rhodesian citizens from holding foreign currency, but the law was widely flouted.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> In order to encourage white emigration, the guerrillas of ZANU and ZAPU followed a strategy of attacking anything and everything that was of economic value across the country in order to force the state to call up more men, and of killing white civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-Brownell_pages_591-610_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brownell_pages_591-610-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Killing Rhodesian white citizens tended to have an "echo effect" as the ZANU and ZAPU had both estimated that for 1 white citizen killed, it caused about 20 to leave Rhodesia.<sup id="cite_ref-Brownell_pages_591-610_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brownell_pages_591-610-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_the_Bush_War">End of the Bush War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: End of the Bush War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:368px;max-width:368px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:RhodesiaAllies1965.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/RhodesiaAllies1965.png/180px-RhodesiaAllies1965.png" width="180" height="137" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/RhodesiaAllies1965.png/270px-RhodesiaAllies1965.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/RhodesiaAllies1965.png/360px-RhodesiaAllies1965.png 2x" data-file-width="624" data-file-height="475" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:RhodesiaAllies1975.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/RhodesiaAllies1975.png/180px-RhodesiaAllies1975.png" width="180" height="137" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/RhodesiaAllies1975.png/270px-RhodesiaAllies1975.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/RhodesiaAllies1975.png/360px-RhodesiaAllies1975.png 2x" data-file-width="624" data-file-height="475" /></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">The geographical situation in 1965 (left, on UDI) and 1975 (right, after the independence of Mozambique and Angola from Portugal). Green: Rhodesia; purple: friendly nations; orange: hostile states; grey: neutral countries.</div></div></div>
<p>Rhodesia began to lose vital economic and military support from South Africa, which, while sympathetic to the white minority government, never accorded it diplomatic recognition. The South African government placed limits on the fuel and munitions they supplied to the Rhodesian military. They also withdrew the personnel and equipment that they had previously provided to aid the war effort, though covert military support continued.<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1976, the South African government and <a href="/wiki/United_States_government" class="mw-redirect" title="United States government">United States governments</a> worked together to place pressure on Smith to agree to a form of majority rule. In response to the initiative of US Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Henry_Kissinger" title="Henry Kissinger">Henry Kissinger</a>, in 1976 Ian Smith accepted the principle of black majority rule within two years.<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The Rhodesians now offered more concessions, but those concessions, focused on reaching an "internal settlement" with moderate black leaders, were insufficient to end the war.
</p><p>At the time, some Rhodesians said the still embittered history between the British-dominated Rhodesia and the <a href="/wiki/Afrikaner" class="mw-redirect" title="Afrikaner">Afrikaner</a>-dominated South Africa partly led the South African government to withdraw its aid to Rhodesia. Ian Smith said in his memoirs that even though many white South Africans supported Rhodesia, South African Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/John_Vorster" title="John Vorster">John Vorster</a>'s policy of <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A9tente" title="Détente">détente</a> with the Black African states ended up with Rhodesia being offered as the "sacrificial lamb" to buy more time for South Africa. Other observers perceived South Africa's distancing itself from Rhodesia as being an early move in the process that led to majority rule in South Africa itself.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>In 1976 South Africa saw settlement of the Rhodesian question as vital on several fronts: to cauterise the wound of the psychological blow … caused by her defeat in the Angolan conflict; to pre-empt possible Cuban intervention in Rhodesia and the possibility of South Africa being sucked into another <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> regional conflict without the support and endorsement of the western powers</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Dr Sue Onslow, South Africa and UDI<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>In the latter 1970s, the militants had successfully put the economy of Rhodesia under significant pressure while the numbers of guerrillas in the country were steadily increasing.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The government abandoned its early strategy of trying to defend the borders in favour of trying to defend key economic areas and lines of communication with South Africa, while the rest of the countryside became a patchwork of "<a href="/wiki/No-go_area" title="No-go area">no-go areas</a>".
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_1970s">Late 1970s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Late 1970s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>By the late 1970s, Rhodesia's front-line forces contained about 25,000 regular troops and police – backed up by relatively strong army and police reserves.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Its mechanised contingent consisted of light armoured cars and improvised <a href="/wiki/MRAP" title="MRAP">mine-protected</a> armoured personnel carriers, complemented by eight tanks (Polish built <a href="/wiki/T-54/T-55_Operators_and_variants#Poland" class="mw-redirect" title="T-54/T-55 Operators and variants">T-55LD</a> tanks), delivered in the last year of the war. The <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_Air_Force" title="Rhodesian Air Force">Rhodesian Air Force</a> operated an assortment of both <a href="/wiki/English_Electric_Canberra" title="English Electric Canberra">Canberra</a> light bombers, <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Hunter" title="Hawker Hunter">Hawker Hunter</a> fighter bombers, older <a href="/wiki/De_Havilland_Vampire" title="De Havilland Vampire">de Havilland Vampire</a> jets as well as a somewhat antiquated, but still potent, helicopter arm. These forces, including highly trained special operations units, were capable of launching devastating raids on resistance movement camps outside the country, as in <a href="/wiki/Operation_Dingo" title="Operation Dingo">Operation Dingo</a> in 1977 and other similar operations.
</p><p>Nevertheless, guerrilla pressure inside the country itself was steadily increasing in the latter 1970s. By 1978–79, the war had become a contest between the guerrilla warfare placing ever increasing pressure on the Rhodesian regime and civil population, and the Rhodesian government's strategy of trying to hold off the militants until external recognition for a compromise political settlement with moderate black leaders could be secured.
