</p><p>By this time, the need to cut a deal was apparent to most Rhodesians, but not to all. Ian Smith had dismissed his intransigent Defence Minister, <a href="/wiki/P._K._van_der_Byl" title="P. K. van der Byl">P. K. van der Byl</a>, as early as 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Van der Byl was a hard-line opponent of any form of compromise with domestic opposition or the international community since before UDI.
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>...it is better to fight to the last man and the last cartridge and die with some honour. Because, what is being presented to us here is a degree of humiliation...</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>P. K. van der Byl in 1977, commenting on a British peace plan.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Van der Byl eventually retired to his country estate outside <a href="/wiki/Cape_Town" title="Cape Town">Cape Town</a>, but there were elements in Rhodesia, mainly embittered former security force personnel, who forcibly opposed majority rule up to and well beyond the establishment of majority rule.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> New white immigrants continued to arrive in Rhodesia right up to the eve of majority rule.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Intensification_of_the_Bush_War">Intensification of the Bush War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Intensification of the Bush War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The work of journalists such as <a href="/wiki/Lord_Richard_Cecil" title="Lord Richard Cecil">Lord Richard Cecil</a>, son of the <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gascoyne-Cecil,_6th_Marquess_of_Salisbury" title="Robert Gascoyne-Cecil, 6th Marquess of Salisbury">Marquess of Salisbury</a>, stiffened the morale of Rhodesians and their overseas supporters.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Lord Richard produced regular news reports such as the Thames TV 'Frontline Rhodesia' features. These reports typically contrasted the incompetent insurgents with the "superbly professional" government troops.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> A group of ZANLA fighters killed Lord Richard on 20 April 1978 when he was accompanying a Rhodesian airborne unit employed in Fire Force Operations.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The shooting down on 3 September 1978 of the civilian <a href="/wiki/Vickers_Viscount" title="Vickers Viscount">Vickers Viscount</a> airliner <i>Hunyani</i>, <a href="/wiki/Air_Rhodesia_Flight_RH825" class="mw-redirect" title="Air Rhodesia Flight RH825">Air Rhodesia Flight RH825</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Kariba,_Zimbabwe" title="Kariba, Zimbabwe">Kariba</a> area by ZIPRA fighters using a <a href="/wiki/Surface-to-air_missile" title="Surface-to-air missile">surface-to-air missile</a>, with the subsequent massacre of its survivors, is widely considered to be the event that finally destroyed the Rhodesians' will to continue the war. Although militarily insignificant, the loss of this aircraft (and a second Viscount, the <i><a href="/wiki/Air_Rhodesia_Flight_827" title="Air Rhodesia Flight 827">Umniati</a></i>, in 1979) demonstrated the reach of resistance movements extended to Rhodesian civil society.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Rhodesians' means to continue the war were also eroding fast. In December 1978, a ZANLA unit penetrated the outskirts of Salisbury and fired a volley of rockets and <a href="/wiki/Incendiary_device" title="Incendiary device">incendiary device</a> rounds into the main oil storage depot – the most heavily defended economic asset in the country. The storage tanks burned for five days, giving off a column of smoke that could be seen 130 kilometres (80&#160;mi) away. Five hundred thousand barrels (79,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of petroleum product (comprising Rhodesia's strategic oil reserve) were lost.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The government's defence spending increased from R$30 million, 8.5% of the national budget in 1971 to 1972, to R$400 m in 1978 to 1979, 47% of the national budget. In 1980, the post-independence government of Zimbabwe inherited a US$500 million national debt.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="End_of_UDI_.281979.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_UDI_(1979)">End of UDI (1979)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: End of UDI (1979)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Internal_Settlement.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Internal_Settlement.jpg/220px-Internal_Settlement.jpg" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Internal_Settlement.jpg/330px-Internal_Settlement.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Internal_Settlement.jpg/440px-Internal_Settlement.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1286" data-file-height="812" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Internal_Settlement.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Signing the Rhodesian Internal Settlement (from left: Bishop Abel Muzorewa, <a href="/wiki/Ian_Smith" title="Ian Smith">Ian Smith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jeremiah_Chirau" title="Jeremiah Chirau">Jeremiah Chirau</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ndabaningi_Sithole" title="Ndabaningi Sithole">Ndabaningi Sithole</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>The Rhodesian army continued its "mobile counter-offensive" strategy of holding key positions ("vital asset ground") while carrying out raids into the no-go areas and into neighbouring countries. While often extraordinarily successful in inflicting heavy guerrilla casualties, such raids also on occasion failed to achieve their objectives. In April 1979 special forces carried out a raid on <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Nkomo" title="Joshua Nkomo">Joshua Nkomo</a>'s residence in <a href="/wiki/Lusaka" title="Lusaka">Lusaka</a> (<a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a>) with the stated intention of assassinating him.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Nkomo and his family left hastily a few hours before the raid – having clearly been warned that the raid was coming.
</p><p>In 1979, some special forces units were accused of using counterinsurgent operations as cover for ivory poaching and smuggling. Colonel <a href="/wiki/Reid-Daly" class="mw-redirect" title="Reid-Daly">Reid-Daly</a> (commander of the <a href="/wiki/Selous_Scouts" title="Selous Scouts">Selous Scouts</a>) discovered that his phone was bugged and after challenging a superior officer on this issue was court martialled for insubordination. He received the lightest sentence possible, a caution, but he continued to fight his conviction and eventually resigned his commission and left the Army.
</p><p>By 1978–79, up to 70% of the regular army was composed of black soldiers (though both the army and police reserves remained overwhelmingly white). By 1979 there were also 30 black commissioned officers in the regular army. While there was never any suggestion of disloyalty among the soldiers from predominantly black units (in particular within the Selous Scouts or the <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_African_Rifles" title="Rhodesian African Rifles">Rhodesian African Rifles</a> – RAR), some argue that, by the time of the 1980 election, many of the RAR soldiers voted for Robert Mugabe.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As the result of an <a href="/wiki/Internal_Settlement" title="Internal Settlement">Internal Settlement</a> signed on 3 March 1978 between the Rhodesian government and the moderate African nationalist parties, which were not in exile and not involved in the war, elections were held in April 1979. The <a href="/wiki/United_African_National_Council" title="United African National Council">United African National Council</a> (UANC) party won a majority in this election, and its leader, <a href="/wiki/Abel_Muzorewa" title="Abel Muzorewa">Abel Muzorewa</a> (a <a href="/wiki/United_Methodist_Church" title="United Methodist Church">United Methodist Church</a> bishop), became the country's first black prime minister on 1 June 1979. The country's name was changed to <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_Rhodesia" title="Zimbabwe Rhodesia">Zimbabwe Rhodesia</a>. The internal settlement left control of the country's police, security forces, civil service and judiciary in white hands, for the moment. It assured whites of about one third of the seats in parliament. It was essentially a power-sharing arrangement between whites and blacks which, in the eyes of many, particularly the insurgents, did not amount to majority rule.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> However, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">United States Senate</a> voted to end economic sanctions against Zimbabwe Rhodesia on 12 June.<sup id="cite_ref-endsanctions_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-endsanctions-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While the 1979 election was described by the Rhodesian government as non-racial and democratic, it did not include the main nationalist parties ZANU and ZAPU. In spite of offers from Ian Smith, the latter parties declined to participate in an election in which their political position would be insecure and under a proposed constitution which they had played no part in drafting and which was perceived as retaining strong white minority privilege.
</p><p>Bishop Muzorewa's government did not receive international recognition. The Bush War continued unabated and sanctions were not lifted. The international community refused to accept the validity of any agreement which did not incorporate the main nationalist parties. The British Government (then led by the recently elected <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a>) issued invitations to all parties to attend a peace conference at <a href="/wiki/Lancaster_House" title="Lancaster House">Lancaster House</a>. These negotiations took place in London in late 1979. The three-month-long conference almost failed to reach conclusion, due to disagreements on <a href="/wiki/Land_reform_in_Zimbabwe" title="Land reform in Zimbabwe">land reform</a>, but resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Lancaster_House_Agreement" title="Lancaster House Agreement">Lancaster House Agreement</a>. UDI ended, and Rhodesia temporarily reverted to the status of a British colony (the 'Colony of Southern Rhodesia').<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> As per the agreement, <a href="/wiki/Lord_Soames" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Soames">Lord Soames</a> became Governor with full legislative and executive powers.
</p><p>The Lancaster House Agreement further provided for a ceasefire which was followed by an internationally supervised general election, held on <a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesian_general_election,_1980" title="Southern Rhodesian general election, 1980">February 1980</a>. ZANU led by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Mugabe" title="Robert Mugabe">Robert Mugabe</a> won this election, some alleged,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (March 2017)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> by terrorising its political opposition, including supporters of ZAPU, through former insurgents that had not confined themselves to the designated guerrilla assembly points, as stipulated by the Lancaster House Agreement. The observers and Soames were accused of looking the other way, and Mugabe's victory was certified. Nevertheless, few could doubt that Mugabe's support within his majority Shona tribal group was extremely strong. The Rhodesian military seriously considered mounting a coup against a perceived stolen election ("Operation Quartz") to prevent ZANU from taking over the country.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The alleged coup was to include the assassination of Mugabe and coordinated assaults on guerrilla assembly points throughout the country. The plan was eventually scuttled, as it was obvious that Mugabe enjoyed widespread support from the black majority despite voter intimidation, as well as the fact that the coup would gain no external support, and a conflagration which would engulf the country was seen as inevitable.
</p>
<h3><span id="Republic_of_Zimbabwe_.281980.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Republic_of_Zimbabwe_(1980)">Republic of Zimbabwe (1980)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Republic of Zimbabwe (1980)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Mugabe and the victorious black nationalists were rather less concerned by Operation Quartz than by the possibility that there might be a mass exodus of the white community of the kind that had caused chaos in Mozambique five years earlier. Such an exodus had been prepared for by the South African government. With the agreement of the British Governor of Rhodesia, South African troops had entered the country to secure the road approaches to the <a href="/wiki/Beit_Bridge" class="mw-redirect" title="Beit Bridge">Beit Bridge</a> border crossing point. Refugee camps had been prepared in the <a href="/wiki/Transvaal_Province" class="mw-redirect" title="Transvaal Province">Transvaal</a>. On the day the election results became known, most white families had prepared contingency plans for flight, including the packing of cars and suitcases.
</p><p>However, after a meeting with Robert Mugabe and the central committee of ZANU (PF), Ian Smith was reassured that whites could and should stay in the new Zimbabwe. Mugabe promised that he would abide strictly by the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Lancaster_House_Agreement" title="Lancaster House Agreement">Lancaster House Agreement</a> and that changes in Zimbabwe would be made gradually and by a proper legal process. In a CBS news interview, Mugabe claimed that Rhodesian whites "...are still in control of the economy, the majority being commercial farmers."<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Mugabe, however, would reverse his commitment to these agreements some years later; the regime began confiscating white-owned farmlands. This is widely blamed for leading to the deterioration of the Zimbabwean economy, which plagues the country today.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 18 April 1980 the country became independent within the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a> as the Republic of Zimbabwe, and its capital, Salisbury, was renamed <a href="/wiki/Harare" title="Harare">Harare</a> two years later.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Rhodesia is equivalent in territory to modern <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>. It was a <a href="/wiki/Landlocked_country" title="Landlocked country">landlocked country</a> in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">southern Africa</a>, lying between latitudes <a href="/wiki/15th_parallel_south" title="15th parallel south">15°</a> and <a href="/wiki/23rd_parallel_south" title="23rd parallel south">23°S</a>, and longitudes <a href="/wiki/25th_meridian_east" title="25th meridian east">25°</a> and <a href="/wiki/34th_meridian_east" title="34th meridian east">34°E</a>. It was bordered by <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> to the south, the <a href="/wiki/Bechuanaland_Protectorate" title="Bechuanaland Protectorate">Bechuanaland Protectorate</a> (later <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a>) to the west and southwest, <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a> to the northwest, and <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> to the east and northeast. Its northwest corner is roughly 150 metres (490 feet) from <a href="/wiki/South_West_Africa" title="South West Africa">South West Africa</a> (present-day <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a>), South Africa, nearly forming a four-nation <a href="/wiki/Quadripoint" title="Quadripoint">quadripoint</a>. Most of the country is elevated, consisting of a central plateau (high veld) stretching from the southwest northwards with altitudes between 1,000 and 1,600&#160;m (3,300 and 5,200&#160;ft). The country's extreme east is mountainous, this area being known as the Eastern Highlands, with <a href="/wiki/Mount_Nyangani" title="Mount Nyangani">Mount Inyangani</a> as the highest point at 2,592&#160;m (8,504&#160;ft).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Zimbabwe" class="mw-redirect" title="Climate of Zimbabwe">Climate of Zimbabwe</a></div>
<p>Rhodesia had a <a href="/wiki/Tropical_climate" title="Tropical climate">tropical climate</a> with many local variations. The southern areas are known for their heat and aridity, parts of the central plateau receive frost in winter, the <a href="/wiki/Zambezi" title="Zambezi">Zambezi</a> valley is also known for its extreme heat and the Eastern Highlands usually experience cool temperatures and the highest rainfall in the country. The country's rainy season was from late October to March and the hot climate is moderated by increasing altitude. The country was faced with recurring droughts, and severe storms are rare.<sup id="cite_ref-Baughan,_M._2005_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baughan,_M._2005-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity">Biodiversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Biodiversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Zimbabwe" title="Wildlife of Zimbabwe">Wildlife of Zimbabwe</a></div>
<p>The country was mostly savannah, although the moist and mountainous eastern highlands support areas of tropical evergreen and hardwood forests. Trees found in these Eastern Highlands included <a href="/wiki/Teak" title="Teak">teak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mahogany" title="Mahogany">mahogany</a>, enormous specimens of <a href="/wiki/Strangler_fig" title="Strangler fig">strangling fig</a>, forest newtonia, big leaf, <a href="/wiki/Celtis_africana" title="Celtis africana">white stinkwood</a>, chirinda stinkwood, <a href="/wiki/Senegalia_nigrescens" title="Senegalia nigrescens">knobthorn</a> and many others.
</p><p>In the low-lying parts of the country fever trees, <a href="/wiki/Mopane" title="Mopane">mopane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Combretum" title="Combretum">combretum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adansonia" title="Adansonia">baobabs</a> abound. Much of the country is covered by <a href="/wiki/Miombo" title="Miombo">miombo</a> woodland, dominated by <a href="/wiki/Brachystegia" title="Brachystegia">brachystegia</a> species and others. Among the numerous flowers and shrubs are <a href="/wiki/Hibiscus" title="Hibiscus">hibiscus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flame_lily" class="mw-redirect" title="Flame lily">flame lily</a>, snake lily, <a href="/wiki/Spider_lily" title="Spider lily">spider lily</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leonotis" title="Leonotis">leonotus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cassia_(genus)" title="Cassia (genus)">cassia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wisteria" title="Wisteria">tree wisteria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dombeya" title="Dombeya">dombeya</a>. There are around 350 species of mammals that can be found in Rhodesia. There are also many snakes and lizards, over 500 bird species, and 131 fish species.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_politics">Government and politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Government and politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</div></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Politics_of_Rhodesia" title="Template:Politics of Rhodesia"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Politics_of_Rhodesia" title="Template talk:Politics of Rhodesia"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Politics_of_Rhodesia&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Rhodesia" title="Politics of Rhodesia">Politics of Rhodesia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_President_of_Rhodesia_(1970-1979).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Flag_of_the_President_of_Rhodesia_%281970-1979%29.svg/240px-Flag_of_the_President_of_Rhodesia_%281970-1979%29.svg.png" width="240" height="120" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Flag_of_the_President_of_Rhodesia_%281970-1979%29.svg/360px-Flag_of_the_President_of_Rhodesia_%281970-1979%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Flag_of_the_President_of_Rhodesia_%281970-1979%29.svg/480px-Flag_of_the_President_of_Rhodesia_%281970-1979%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_President_of_Rhodesia_(1970-1979).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Presidential flag of Rhodesia</div></div></div>
<p>Although Southern Rhodesia never gained full <a href="/wiki/Dominion" title="Dominion">dominion</a> status within the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>, Southern Rhodesians ruled themselves from the attainment of '<a href="/wiki/Responsible_government" title="Responsible government">Responsible Government</a>' in 1923. Its electoral register had property and education qualifications. Over the years various electoral arrangements made at a national and municipal level upheld these standards. For example, the franchise for the first <a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesian_Legislative_Council" title="Southern Rhodesian Legislative Council">Southern Rhodesian Legislative Council</a> election in 1899 contained the following requirement:
</p>
<blockquote><p>voters to be British subjects, male, 21 years of age and older, able to write their address and occupation, and then to fulfil the following financial requirements: (a) ownership of a registered mining claim in Southern Rhodesia, or (b) occupying immovable property worth £75, or (c) receiving wages or salary of £50 per annum in Southern Rhodesia. Six months' continuous residence was also required for qualifications (b) and (c).</p></blockquote>
<p>Following <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Rhodes" title="Cecil Rhodes">Cecil Rhodes</a>'s dictum of "equal rights for all civilised men", there was no overt racial component to the franchise. However, the requirement excluded a majority of native blacks from the electorate.
</p><p>Up until the 1950s, Southern Rhodesia had a vibrant political life with right and left wing parties competing for power. The <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_Labour_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhodesian Labour Party">Rhodesian Labour Party</a> held seats in the Assembly and in municipal councils throughout the 1920s and 1930s. From 1953 to 1958, the prime minister was <a href="/wiki/Garfield_Todd" title="Garfield Todd">Garfield Todd</a>, a liberal who did much to promote the development of the Black community through investment in education, housing and healthcare. However, the government forced Todd from office because his proposed reforms were seen by many whites as too radical.
</p><p>From 1958 onwards, white settler politics consolidated and ossified around resistance to majority rule, setting the stage for UDI. The 1961 Constitution governed Southern Rhodesia and independent Rhodesia up until 1969, using the Westminster Parliamentary System modified by a system of separate voter rolls with differing property and education qualifications, without regard to race. Whites ended up with the majority of Assembly seats.
</p><p>The 1969 republican constitution established a <a href="/wiki/Bicameral" class="mw-redirect" title="Bicameral">bicameral</a> Parliament consisting of an indirectly elected <a href="/wiki/Senate" title="Senate">Senate</a> and a directly elected <a href="/wiki/House_of_Assembly" title="House of Assembly">House of Assembly</a>, effectively reserving the majority of seats for whites. The office of <a href="/wiki/President_of_Rhodesia" title="President of Rhodesia">President</a> had only ceremonial significance with the Prime Minister holding executive power.
</p><p>The Constitution of the short-lived <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_Rhodesia" title="Zimbabwe Rhodesia">Zimbabwe Rhodesia</a>, which saw a black-led government elected for the first time, reserved 28 of the 100 parliamentary seats for whites. The independence constitution agreed at <a href="/wiki/Lancaster_House_Agreement" title="Lancaster House Agreement">Lancaster House</a> watered those provisions down and reserved 20 out of 100 seats for whites in the House of Assembly and 8 out of 40 seats in the Senate. The constitution prohibited Zimbabwe authorities from altering the Constitution for seven years without unanimous consent and required a three-quarters vote in Parliament for a further three years. The government amended the Constitution in 1987 to abolish the seats reserved for whites, and replace the office of Prime Minister with an executive President. In 1990, the government abolished the Senate.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_Security_Forces" title="Rhodesian Security Forces">Rhodesian Security Forces</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rhodesian_Eland_and_T-55.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Rhodesian_Eland_and_T-55.jpg/260px-Rhodesian_Eland_and_T-55.jpg" width="260" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Rhodesian_Eland_and_T-55.jpg/390px-Rhodesian_Eland_and_T-55.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Rhodesian_Eland_and_T-55.jpg/520px-Rhodesian_Eland_and_T-55.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1385" data-file-height="937" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rhodesian_Eland_and_T-55.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Troops of the <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_Armoured_Corps" title="Rhodesian Armoured Corps">Rhodesian Armoured Corps</a> in 1979.</div></div></div>
<p>Southern Rhodesia had long been distinctive among British dependencies in that it had financed and developed its own security forces and command structure.<sup id="cite_ref-Good_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> After UDI, this posed a particular dilemma for the British government, which considered and rejected various proposals aimed at ending Rhodesia's state of rebellion by force.<sup id="cite_ref-Good_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Harold Wilson once remarked that bringing an end to Rhodesian independence "would not be a case of arresting a subversive individual. It would mean a bloody war, and probably a bloody war turning into a bloody civil war."<sup id="cite_ref-Good_125-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> The formidable nature of the Rhodesian security forces, as well as British fears of a direct South African intervention on behalf of the rogue colony, preempted the further consideration of military options.<sup id="cite_ref-Good_125-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For much of its history Rhodesia had a small professional standing army of 3,400 troops, about a third of whom were black volunteers.<sup id="cite_ref-Good_125-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> The troops were organised into light infantry battalions optimised for counter-insurgency and unconventional warfare,<sup id="cite_ref-Arnold_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arnold-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> and they possessed little artillery or armour.<sup id="cite_ref-Good_125-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Rhodesian_Air_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Rhodesian Air Force">Royal Rhodesian Air Force</a> had 1,000 personnel and six squadrons of aircraft, including forty to fifty <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Hunter" title="Hawker Hunter">Hawker Hunter</a> and <a href="/wiki/De_Havilland_Vampire" title="De Havilland Vampire">de Havilland Vampire</a> strike aircraft and <a href="/wiki/English_Electric_Canberra" title="English Electric Canberra">English Electric Canberra</a> light bombers.<sup id="cite_ref-Good_125-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> It also possessed a helicopter squadron, a transport squadron, and a light reconnaissance squadron.<sup id="cite_ref-Good_125-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> The Rhodesian military was backed by the <a href="/wiki/British_South_Africa_Police" title="British South Africa Police">British South Africa Police</a> (BSAP), a well-equipped police force whose title was derived from the law enforcement division of the British South Africa Company.<sup id="cite_ref-Good_125-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> The BSAP had armoured vehicles of its own and a potent paramilitary capability.<sup id="cite_ref-Good_125-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Domestic and external intelligence gathering were vested in the <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Organisation" title="Central Intelligence Organisation">Central Intelligence Organisation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-COIN_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-COIN-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As a result of the escalating rural insurgency, the Rhodesian Security Forces began to depend more heavily on white conscripts and reservists of the Territorial Force and Territorial reserves.<sup id="cite_ref-Arnold_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arnold-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Regular units remained small throughout the Rhodesian Bush War but became increasingly specialised and were often able to have an effect utterly disproportionate to their size.<sup id="cite_ref-mcnab2002_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcnab2002-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The security forces included a disproportionate number of personnel who had seen action with the British during the <a href="/wiki/First_Malayan_Emergency" class="mw-redirect" title="First Malayan Emergency">First Malayan Emergency</a> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Aden_Emergency" title="Aden Emergency">Aden Emergency</a>, and their influence gave Rhodesia's defence establishment a solid grounding in <a href="/wiki/Counter-insurgency" title="Counter-insurgency">counter-insurgency</a> warfare and small unit tactics in particular.<sup id="cite_ref-COIN_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-COIN-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, the vastness of the operational area and Rhodesia's limited manpower pool left the army, air force, and BSAP constantly overstretched.<sup id="cite_ref-COIN_127-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-COIN-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Budgetary and resource restraints, coupled with manpower shortages, meant the security forces could not expand quickly enough to match the guerrilla movements, and were almost always outnumbered.<sup id="cite_ref-COIN_127-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-COIN-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Rhodesian units compensated for their disadvantage in this regard by pursuing an aggressive preemptive and counterstrike strategy, raiding neighbouring states to destroy guerrilla forces in their external sanctuaries.<sup id="cite_ref-COIN_127-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-COIN-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>All male Rhodesian citizens aged eighteen to twenty-three, except blacks, were obligated to fulfill four and a half months (later extended to nine months) of full-time <a href="/wiki/National_service" title="National service">national service</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Good_125-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> This was followed by a three-year reservist obligation.<sup id="cite_ref-Good_125-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> By 1974 the national service intakes had been doubled, and whites over twenty-three were also conscripted.<sup id="cite_ref-Arnold_126-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arnold-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> In 1978 the Rhodesian Army had about 14,000 white national servicemen, but continued manpower shortages forced it to recruit black volunteers in larger numbers and extend compulsory military service to all white males up to sixty years of age.<sup id="cite_ref-Arnold_126-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arnold-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> By the end of the Rhodesian Bush War virtually all male white Rhodesians were either serving in the military or police in a full-time or part-time capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-Arnold_126-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arnold-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> The size of the Rhodesian Army had swelled to about 20,000 personnel, and the BSAP to over 40,000, including reservists.<sup id="cite_ref-Arnold_126-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arnold-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_and_chemical_warfare">Biological and chemical warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Biological and chemical warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction" title="Rhodesia and weapons of mass destruction">Rhodesia and weapons of mass destruction</a></div>
<p>From 1975 to 1980 the Rhodesian government made several attempts to weaponise chemical and biological agents.<sup id="cite_ref-Glenn_Cross_1980_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glenn_Cross_1980-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Members of the security forces contaminated supplies before replacing them in guerrilla caches or planted them in rural stores to be stolen by the guerrillas during raids.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> They also poisoned water sources along known infiltration routes along the Rhodesian border, forcing their opponents to travel through more arid regions or carry more water during their treks.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The chemical agents most used in the Rhodesian chemical and biological warfare (CBW) programme were <a href="/wiki/Parathion" title="Parathion">parathion</a> (an organophosphate insecticide) and <a href="/wiki/Thallium" title="Thallium">thallium</a> (a heavy metal commonly found in rodenticide).<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> The weapons the Rhodesians selected for use also included <i>Vibrio cholerae</i> (causative agent of <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a>) and possibly <i>Bacillus anthracis</i> (causative agent of <a href="/wiki/Anthrax" title="Anthrax">anthrax</a>). They also looked at using <i>Rickettsia prowazekii</i> (causative agent of epidemic <a href="/wiki/Typhus" title="Typhus">typhus</a>), and <i>Salmonella typhi</i> (causative agent of <a href="/wiki/Typhoid_fever" title="Typhoid fever">typhoid fever</a>), and toxins—such as <a href="/wiki/Ricin" title="Ricin">ricin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Botulinum" class="mw-redirect" title="Botulinum">botulinum</a> toxin.<sup id="cite_ref-Glenn_Cross_1980_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glenn_Cross_1980-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Biological agents, namely <i>Vibrio cholerae</i> (causative agent of <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a>), had some impact on the fighting capability of ZANLA.<sup id="cite_ref-the_plague_wars_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-the_plague_wars-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Some former officers of the Rhodesian Security Forces alleged that anthrax was used covertly during the late 1970s, but this has been disputed.<sup id="cite_ref-Glenn_Cross_1980_129-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glenn_Cross_1980-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Use of <i>anthracis</i>, ricin, or botulinum toxin was favoured during assassination attempts of prominent guerrilla commanders.<sup id="cite_ref-Glenn_Cross_1980_129-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glenn_Cross_1980-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_Zimbabwe" title="Economic history of Zimbabwe">Economic history of Zimbabwe</a></div>
<p>Economically, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesia" title="Southern Rhodesia">Southern Rhodesia</a> developed an economy that was narrowly based on production of a few primary products, notably, chrome and tobacco. It was therefore vulnerable to the economic cycle. The deep recession of the 1930s gave way to a post-war boom. This boom prompted the immigration of about 200,000 whites between 1945 and 1970, taking the white population up to 307,000. A large number of these immigrants were of British working-class origin, with others coming from the Belgian Congo, Kenya, Tanzania, and later Angola and Mozambique. They established a relatively balanced economy, transforming what was once a primary producer dependent on backwoods farming into an industrial giant which spawned a strong manufacturing sector, iron and steel industries, and modern mining ventures. These economic successes owed little to foreign aid.
</p><p>The economy of the state of Rhodesia sustained international sanctions for a decade following the declaration of its independence, a resistance which waned as more southern African states declared independence and majority rule as well as the destruction of the <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_Bush_War" title="Rhodesian Bush War">Rhodesian Bush War</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population">Population</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Population">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A central feature of the white community in Rhodesia was its transience, as white settlers were just as likely to leave Rhodesia after a few years rather than permanently settle; for example, of the 700 British settlers who were the first white settlers, arriving in 1890, only 15 were still living in Rhodesia in 1924.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> As the white population of Rhodesia had a low birth rate (18 per 1,000 compared to the African rate of 48 per 1,000<sup id="cite_ref-Paxton_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paxton-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>), to maintain white population growth was largely dependent upon taking in new white immigrants with immigration accounting for 60% of the growth of the white Rhodesian population between 1955–72.<sup id="cite_ref-Brownell_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brownell-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> However, the American historian Josiah Brownell noted that the turnover rate for white residents in Rhodesia was very high, as Rhodesia took in a total of 255,692 white immigrants between 1955–79 while the same period a total of 246,583 whites emigrated.<sup id="cite_ref-Brownell_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brownell-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Even during the boom years of the late 1950s, when Rhodesia took in an average of 13,666 white immigrants per year, mostly from the United Kingdom and South Africa, an average of about 7,666 whites emigrated annually.<sup id="cite_ref-Brownell_136-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brownell-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Between 1961–65, Rhodesia took in an average of 8,225 white immigrants per year while also having an average white emigration of 12, 912 per year.<sup id="cite_ref-Brownell_136-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brownell-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Many prospective white immigrants in Rhodesia arrived seeking economic opportunities and departed with fluctuations in the security situation as the Bush War intensified.<sup id="cite_ref-Brownell_136-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brownell-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> A substantial number were uninterested in settling there permanently and did not apply for Rhodesian citizenship, despite a much-publicised 1967 campaign urging them to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-Brownell_136-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brownell-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Brownell asserted that patriotism in the white community was "shallow" due to its essentially expatriate character.<sup id="cite_ref-Brownell_136-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brownell-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Brownell also claimed that the majority of white immigrants in the late 1960s and early 1970s were unskilled laborers who competed with the country's black African workforce and did not contribute badly needed technical or professional skills to the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Brownell_136-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brownell-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> He argued that this was due to a government policy aimed at making white immigration as "unselective as possible" and guaranteeing every white immigrant a job.<sup id="cite_ref-Brownell_136-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brownell-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The population of Rhodesia boomed during the late 1960s due to immigration and an exceptional rate of natural increase among its black citizens, the highest in sub-Saharan Africa at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-Brownell_136-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brownell-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Zimbabwe" title="Languages of Zimbabwe">Languages of Zimbabwe</a></div>
<p>White Rhodesians mostly spoke <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, with a minority that spoke <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans" title="Afrikaans">Afrikaans</a>. Approximately 70% of black Rhodesians spoke <a href="/wiki/Shona_language" title="Shona language">Shona</a>, and around 20% are <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ndebele_people" title="Northern Ndebele people">Ndebele</a> and spoke <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ndebele_language" title="Northern Ndebele language">isiNdebele</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Zimbabwe" title="Religion in Zimbabwe">Religion in Zimbabwe</a></div>
<p>Rhodesia was a predominantly <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> country.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Foreign relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <small><i>(February 2011)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Throughout the period of its <a href="/wiki/Unilateral_Declaration_of_Independence_(Rhodesia)" class="mw-redirect" title="Unilateral Declaration of Independence (Rhodesia)">Unilateral Declaration of Independence</a> (1965 to 1979), Rhodesia pursued a foreign policy of attempting to secure recognition as an independent country, and insisting that its political system would include 'gradual steps to majority rule.' Ardently anti-communist, Rhodesia tried to present itself to the West as a front-line state against communist expansion in Africa, to little avail.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Rhodesia received little international recognition during its existence; recognition only occurred after elections in 1980 and a transition to majority rule.
</p><p>Rhodesia wished to retain its economic prosperity and also feared communist elements in the rebel forces, and thus felt their policy of a gradual progression to black majority rule was justified. However, the international community refused to accept this rationale, believing that their policies were perpetuating racism. This attitude was part of the larger <a href="/wiki/Decolonisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Decolonisation">decolonisation</a> context, during which Western powers such as the United Kingdom, France, and Belgium hastened to grant independence to their colonies in Africa.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_UK_and_the_UDI">The UK and the UDI</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: The UK and the UDI">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Rhodesia was originally a <a href="/wiki/British_colony" class="mw-redirect" title="British colony">British colony</a>. Although <a href="/wiki/Decolonisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Decolonisation">decolonisation</a> in Africa had begun after World War II, it began accelerating in the early 1960s, causing Britain to negotiate independence rapidly with several of its colonies. During this period, it adopted a foreign policy called <a href="/wiki/NIBMAR" class="mw-redirect" title="NIBMAR">NIBMAR</a>, or No Independence Before Majority African Rule, mandating democratic reforms that placed governance in the hands of the majority black Africans. The governing white minority of Rhodesia, led by Ian Smith, opposed the policy and its implications. On 11 November 1965, Rhodesia's minority white government made a <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia%27s_Unilateral_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Rhodesia&#39;s Unilateral Declaration of Independence">unilateral declaration of independence</a> (UDI) from the United Kingdom, as it became apparent that negotiations would not lead to independence under the white regime.
</p><p>The United Kingdom government immediately brought in legislation (Southern Rhodesia Act 1965) which formally abolished all Rhodesian government institutions. This move made life difficult for Rhodesian citizens who wished to travel internationally as passports issued by Rhodesia's UDI administration were not recognised as valid;<sup id="cite_ref-Passports_invalidated_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Passports_invalidated-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> in January 1966, the British issued a statement accepting as valid any passport issued before the declaration of independence and allowing six-month United Kingdom passports to be granted when they expired – provided that the bearer declared they did not intend to aid the UDI Rhodesian government.<sup id="cite_ref-1966_statement_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1966_statement-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Until late 1969, Rhodesia still recognised <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabeth II of the United Kingdom">Queen Elizabeth II</a> as head of state, even though it opposed the British government itself for hindering its goals of independence. The Queen, however, refused to accept the title <i>Queen of Rhodesia.</i> Eventually, the Smith government abandoned attempts to remain loyal to the British Crown, and in 1969, a majority of the electorate voted in referendum to declare Rhodesia a republic. They hoped that this move would facilitate recognition as an independent state by the international community, but the issues of white minority control remained and hindered this effort, and like the UDI before it, the proclamation of a republic lacked international recognition.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sanctions">Sanctions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Sanctions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the declaration of independence, and indeed for the entire duration of its existence, Rhodesia did not receive official recognition from any state, although it did maintain diplomatic relations with South Africa, which was then under <a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">apartheid</a>. South Africa did not recognise Rhodesia to preserve its fragile positions with other nations, but frequently assisted the Rhodesian state. Portugal maintained informal relations until the <a href="/wiki/Carnation_Revolution" title="Carnation Revolution">Carnation Revolution</a> of 1974. The day following the declaration of independence, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a> passed a resolution (<a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_216" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 216">S/RES/216</a>) calling upon all states not to accord Rhodesia recognition, and to refrain from any assistance. The Security Council also imposed selective mandatory economic sanctions, which were later made comprehensive.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, South Africa, Portugal, and <a href="/wiki/Pahlavi_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Pahlavi Dynasty">Iran</a> did not comply with economic sanctions against Rhodesia.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> The US, despite voting in favor of the sanctions at the UNSC, violated them to buy chrome ore from Rhodesia.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Kenneth Kaunda, president of Zambia, also accused western oil companies of violating the sanctions and selling oil to Rhodesia.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_perspective">International perspective</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: International perspective">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Rhodesia's Unilateral Declaration of Independence from the United Kingdom on 11 November 1965 was promptly condemned by the international community. The United Nations Security Council Resolution 216 of 12 November 1965 called "upon all States not to recognise this illegal racist minority regime in Southern Rhodesia."<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Rhodesia campaigned for international acceptance and invoked the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Non-intervention" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-intervention">non-intervention</a> in internal affairs as justification for rebuking external criticism of its internal policies. However, the emerging doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Self-determination" title="Self-determination">self-determination</a> in colonial situations meant that most nations regarded Rhodesia's self-declared independence as illegitimate.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a>, formerly <a href="/wiki/Northern_Rhodesia" title="Northern Rhodesia">Northern Rhodesia</a>, took a pragmatic approach towards Rhodesia. <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Kaunda" title="Kenneth Kaunda">Kenneth Kaunda</a>, heavily dependent on access through Rhodesia for his nation's copper ore exports, fuel, and power imports unofficially worked with the Rhodesian government. Rhodesia still allowed Zambia to export and import its goods through its territory to Mozambique ports, despite the Zambian government's official policy of hostility and non-recognition of the post-UDI Smith Administration.
</p><p>The United States, like all other Western nations, refused to recognise Rhodesia, but unlike others allowed its <a href="/wiki/Consulate-General" class="mw-redirect" title="Consulate-General">Consulate-General</a> to function as a communications conduit between the US government in Washington, DC and the Rhodesian government in Salisbury. When Rhodesia set up an information office in Washington, DC, <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_American_States" title="Organization of American States">OAS</a> nations loudly protested. The US government responded by saying the Rhodesian mission and its staff had no official diplomatic status and violated no US laws.
</p><p>Portugal pursued a middle path with Rhodesia. While not officially recognising Rhodesia under Ian Smith, the government of <a href="/wiki/Ant%C3%B3nio_Salazar" class="mw-redirect" title="António Salazar">António Salazar</a> did permit Rhodesia to establish a <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_mission_in_Lisbon" title="Rhodesian mission in Lisbon">representative mission</a> in Lisbon, and permitted Rhodesian exports and imports through their colony of Mozambique. The Portuguese government in power at that time, authoritarian and ardently anti-communist, gave active behind-the-scenes support in Rhodesia's fight against the guerrilla groups.
</p><p>South Africa, itself under international pressure as a white minority government, pursued a policy of <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A9tente" title="Détente">détente</a> with the black African states at the time. These states wanted South Africa to pressure Ian Smith to accept a faster transition to majority rule in Rhodesia, in return for pledges of non-interference in South Africa's internal affairs. Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/John_Vorster" title="John Vorster">John Vorster</a>, believing majority rule in Rhodesia would lead to international acceptance for South Africa, used a number of tactics to pressure Smith. The South African government held up shipments of fuel and ammunition and pulled out friendly South African forces from Rhodesia. The combined loss of Mozambique and the loss of support from South Africa dealt critical blows to the Rhodesian government.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diplomatic_relations">Diplomatic relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Diplomatic relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the UDI, Rhodesia maintained several overseas missions, including <a href="/wiki/Pretoria" title="Pretoria">Pretoria</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> and until 1975, <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a> in <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Louren%C3%A7o_Marques" class="mw-redirect" title="Lourenço Marques">Lourenço Marques</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Maputo" title="Maputo">Maputo</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since 1961, Rhodesia had an "Accredited Diplomatic Representative" with <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, heading a "Rhodesian Diplomatic Mission" or <a href="/wiki/De_facto_embassy" title="De facto embassy"><i>de facto</i> embassy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Before South Africa left the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a> that year, the then <a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesia" title="Southern Rhodesia">Southern Rhodesia</a> had exchanged High Commissioners with the then <a href="/wiki/Union_of_South_Africa" title="Union of South Africa">Union of South Africa</a>, but following the change in status, the Republic now had a "South African Diplomatic Mission" in <a href="/wiki/Harare" title="Harare">Salisbury</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-lloyd_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lloyd-151">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During 1965, the government of Rhodesia made moves to establish a <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_mission_in_Lisbon" title="Rhodesian mission in Lisbon">mission in Lisbon</a> separate from the British Embassy, with its own accredited representative, having previously been able to establish its own consulate in Lourenço Marques, capital of <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Mozambique" title="Portuguese Mozambique">Portuguese Mozambique</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> This prompted protests from the British government, which was determined that the representative, Harry Reedman, should be a nominal member of the British Ambassador's staff.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> For their part, the Portuguese authorities sought a compromise whereby they would accept Reedman as an independent representative but deny him diplomatic status.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Rhodesian Information Office in <a href="/wiki/Washington_DC" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington DC">Washington</a> remained open following UDI, but its director, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ken_Towsey&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ken Towsey (page does not exist)">Ken Towsey</a>, and his staff were deprived of their diplomatic status.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Previously, there had been a "Minister for Rhodesian Affairs" operating under the aegis of the <a href="/wiki/British_Embassy_in_Washington" class="mw-redirect" title="British Embassy in Washington">British Embassy in Washington</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> as well representatives in <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Following the country's independence as <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>, Towsey became chargé d'affaires at the new Embassy.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The High Commission in London, known as <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia_House" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhodesia House">Rhodesia House</a>, continued to function until it was closed in 1969 following the decision by white Rhodesians in a <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_constitutional_referendum,_1969" title="Rhodesian constitutional referendum, 1969">referendum</a> to make the country a republic, along with the "British Residual Mission" in <a href="/wiki/Harare" title="Harare">Salisbury</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Prior to its closure, the mission flew the newly adopted <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Rhodesia" title="Flag of Rhodesia">Flag of Rhodesia</a>, considered illegal by the <a href="/wiki/Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Office" title="Foreign and Commonwealth Office">Foreign Office</a>, prompting calls by <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a> MP <a href="/wiki/Willie_Hamilton" title="Willie Hamilton">Willie Hamilton</a> for its removal.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, the federal government in <a href="/wiki/Canberra" title="Canberra">Canberra</a> sought to close the Rhodesian Information Centre in <a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">Sydney</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> but it remained open, operating under the jurisdiction of the state of <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales" title="New South Wales">New South Wales</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> In 1973, the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Labor_Party" title="Australian Labor Party">Labor</a> government of <a href="/wiki/Gough_Whitlam" title="Gough Whitlam">Gough Whitlam</a> cut post and telephone links to the Centre, but this was ruled illegal by the <a href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Australia" title="High Court of Australia">High Court</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> An office was also established in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>, but this was closed down by the <a href="/wiki/French_government" class="mw-redirect" title="French government">French government</a> in 1977.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Similarly, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> recalled its consul-general from <a href="/wiki/Harare" title="Harare">Salisbury</a>, and reduced consular staff,<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> but did not move to close its consulate until the declaration of a republic in 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, however, retained its "Accredited Diplomatic Representative" after UDI,<sup id="cite_ref-google13_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google13-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> which allowed it to continue to recognise <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">British</a> sovereignty as well as to deal with the <i>de facto</i> authority of the government of <a href="/wiki/Ian_Smith" title="Ian Smith">Ian Smith</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-google257_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google257-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The South African Diplomatic Mission in Salisbury became the only such mission remaining in the country after 1975,<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> when <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> downgraded its mission to consul level,<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> having recalled its consul-general in Salisbury in May 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> After Zimbabwe's independence, the new government closed its missions in <a href="/wiki/Pretoria" title="Pretoria">Pretoria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cape_Town" title="Cape Town">Cape Town</a>, only maintaining a trade mission in <a href="/wiki/Johannesburg" title="Johannesburg">Johannesburg</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> while the South African Diplomatic Mission in Salisbury was also closed.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Results">Results</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Results">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Continuing civil war and a lack of international support eventually led the Rhodesian government to submit to an agreement with the UK in 1979. This led to internationally supervised elections, won by <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_African_National_Union_-_Patriotic_Front" class="mw-redirect" title="Zimbabwe African National Union - Patriotic Front">Zimbabwe African National Union - Patriotic Front</a> and Robert Mugabe, establishing the internationally recognised <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the ten years after independence, around 60% of the white population of Zimbabwe emigrated, most to South Africa and to other mainly white, English speaking countries where they formed expatriate communities. Politically within Zimbabwe, the consolidation of power by Robert Mugabe continued through the 1980s. Following amendments to the country's constitution in 1987, parliamentary seats reserved for whites were abolished, and an executive presidency was created, held by Mugabe. Many expatriates and some of the whites who stayed in Zimbabwe became deeply nostalgic for Rhodesia. These individuals are known as "<a href="/wiki/Rhodie" title="Rhodie">Rhodies</a>." Native whites who are more accepting of the new order are known as "Zimbos."
</p><p>While as Rhodesia, the country was once considered the <a href="/wiki/Breadbasket" title="Breadbasket">breadbasket</a> of Africa. Today, Zimbabwe is a net importer of foodstuffs, with the European Union and United States providing emergency food relief as humanitarian aid on a regular basis.<sup id="cite_ref-cia.gov_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia.gov-172">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> The nation has suffered profound economic and social decline in the past twenty years. Recently the agriculture sector has started to do well since the availability of expertise and machines has improved supported mainly by China.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Zimbabwe also suffered from a crippling inflation rate, as the <a href="/wiki/Reserve_Bank_of_Zimbabwe" title="Reserve Bank of Zimbabwe">Reserve Bank of Zimbabwe</a> had a policy of printing money to satisfy government debt. This policy caused the inflation rate to increase from 32% in 1998 to 11,200,000% in 2007. Monetary aid by the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> was suspended due to the Zimbabwe government's defaulting on past loans, its inability to stabilise its own economy, its inability to stem corruption and its failure to advance human rights.<sup id="cite_ref-cia.gov_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia.gov-172">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> In 2009, Zimbabwe abandoned its currency, relying instead on foreign currencies.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 2008 elections, Mugabe garnered 41%, Simba Makoni 10% and <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Tsvangirai" title="Morgan Tsvangirai">Morgan Tsvangirai</a> 48% of the votes cast for president, forcing a runoff election called by the Zimbabwe Electoral Commission (ZEC). In the months leading to the run-off, instances of extreme violence between the two major parties (ZANU PF and MDC) led Tsvangirai to withdraw from the election. In February 2009, a power-sharing accord was reached which resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_Government_of_National_Unity_of_2009" title="Zimbabwe Government of National Unity of 2009">Zimbabwe Government of National Unity of 2009</a>. The accord was, essentially, to create the position of "Prime Minister" for Tsvangirai, who served in that role from 2009 to 2013. Mugabe retained the title of President.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The main newspapers were the <i><a href="/wiki/Rhodesia_Herald" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhodesia Herald">Rhodesia Herald</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Harare" title="Harare">Salisbury</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Chronicle_(Zimbabwe)" title="The Chronicle (Zimbabwe)">The Chronicle</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Bulawayo" title="Bulawayo">Bulawayo</a>. Following UDI, in 1976, the state-run <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="Zimbabwe Broadcasting Corporation">Rhodesian Broadcasting Corporation (RBC)</a> took over the privately owned <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_Broadcasting_Corporation#Television" title="Zimbabwe Broadcasting Corporation">Rhodesian Television (RTV)</a> service, in which it had previously acquired a 51 per cent stake.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>
Among the news magazines published in Rhodesia under UDI were the <i><a href="/wiki/Illustrated_Life_Rhodesia" title="Illustrated Life Rhodesia">Illustrated Life Rhodesia</a></i>, while <i>The Valiant Years</i> by Beryl Salt told the history of Rhodesia from 1890 to 1978 entirely through the medium of facsimile reproduction of articles and headlines from Rhodesian newspapers.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rhodesia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Sports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since Rhodesia was a former colony of the United Kingdom, all of the sports that were born in the United Kingdom enjoyed humongous popularity in Rhodesia; including <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">cricket</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rugby_football" title="Rugby football">rugby</a>, <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a> (<i>soccer</i>), <a href="/wiki/Netball" title="Netball">netball</a>, <a href="/wiki/Golf" title="Golf">golf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tennis" title="Tennis">tennis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lawn_bowls" class="mw-redirect" title="Lawn bowls">lawn bowls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Field_hockey" title="Field hockey">field hockey</a>, etc. Just like neighbouring South Africa, Rhodesia was barred from both competeing against and participating with <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a> member countries.
</p>
